## Chapter 5: Discrete Choice:




### Introduction

Queues are a fundamental concept in logistical and transportation planning. They are used to model and analyze systems where customers or vehicles arrive, wait in line, and are eventually served. This chapter will provide an introduction to queues, covering the basic concepts and principles that are essential for understanding and applying queueing theory in logistical and transportation planning.

Queueing theory is a mathematical discipline that studies the behavior of systems where customers or vehicles arrive, wait in line, and are eventually served. It is a powerful tool for analyzing and optimizing systems that involve queuing, such as traffic flow, supply chain management, and customer service.

In this chapter, we will start by defining what a queue is and how it is modeled mathematically. We will then discuss the different types of queues, including single-server queues, multiple-server queues, and queues with multiple queues. We will also cover the key performance measures of queues, such as average queue length, average waiting time, and average number of customers in the system.

We will also introduce the concept of queue discipline, which determines the order in which customers or vehicles are served. Common queue disciplines include first-come-first-served, last-come-first-served, and priority-based.

Finally, we will discuss the applications of queueing theory in logistical and transportation planning. We will explore how queueing theory can be used to model and analyze traffic flow, supply chain operations, and customer service systems. We will also discuss how queueing theory can be used to optimize these systems and improve their performance.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid understanding of queues and their role in logistical and transportation planning. They will also be equipped with the necessary knowledge to apply queueing theory to real-world problems and make informed decisions about system design and optimization.




### Subsection: 5.1a Definition of Queues

A queue is a fundamental concept in logistical and transportation planning. It is a system where customers or vehicles arrive, wait in line, and are eventually served. Queues are used to model and analyze a wide range of systems, including traffic flow, supply chain management, and customer service.

Mathematically, a queue is a discrete event simulation model that describes the arrival, service, and departure of customers or vehicles. The arrival process describes how customers or vehicles enter the queue, the service process describes how they are served, and the departure process describes how they leave the queue.

The behavior of a queue is determined by a set of parameters, including the arrival rate, the service rate, and the queue discipline. The arrival rate is the average number of customers or vehicles that arrive at the queue per unit time. The service rate is the average number of customers or vehicles that can be served per unit time. The queue discipline determines the order in which customers or vehicles are served.

There are several types of queues, including single-server queues, multiple-server queues, and queues with multiple queues. In a single-server queue, there is only one server that serves all the customers or vehicles. In a multiple-server queue, there are multiple servers that serve the customers or vehicles. In a queue with multiple queues, there are multiple queues that serve different types of customers or vehicles.

The performance of a queue is measured by several key performance measures, including the average queue length, the average waiting time, and the average number of customers or vehicles in the system. These measures provide valuable insights into the behavior of the queue and can be used to optimize the queue and improve its performance.

In the next section, we will introduce the concept of Little's Law, which is a fundamental principle in queueing theory that relates the average queue length, the average waiting time, and the arrival and service rates of a queue.




### Subsection: 5.1b Littles Law and its Implications

Little's Law is a fundamental principle in queueing theory that relates the average number of customers in a queue, the average arrival rate, and the average service rate. It is named after John Little, who first formulated the law in the 1960s. Little's Law is a powerful tool for analyzing queueing systems and predicting their behavior.

#### The Statement of Little's Law

Little's Law can be stated in several equivalent ways. One of the most common formulations is:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the queue, $\lambda$ is the average arrival rate, and $W$ is the average time a customer spends in the queue.

This formulation of Little's Law can be understood as a balance equation. The left-hand side represents the number of customers in the queue, which is the result of the arrival process. The right-hand side represents the number of customers served per unit time, which is the result of the service process. Since these two processes must balance, the equation holds.

Another formulation of Little's Law is:

$$
L = \lambda \cdot \text{E}[W]
$$

where $\text{E}[W]$ is the expected value of the waiting time. This formulation emphasizes the role of the arrival rate and the expected waiting time in determining the average number of customers in the queue.

#### Implications of Little's Law

Little's Law has several important implications for queueing systems. One of the most significant implications is that it provides a way to calculate the average waiting time in a queue. Since Little's Law relates the average number of customers in the queue, the average arrival rate, and the average waiting time, if we know two of these quantities, we can calculate the third.

Another implication of Little's Law is that it provides a way to analyze the stability of a queueing system. A queueing system is stable if the average number of customers in the queue is finite. According to Little's Law, a queueing system is stable if the arrival rate is less than or equal to the service rate.

Little's Law also has implications for the design and management of queueing systems. For example, it suggests that increasing the service rate is an effective way to reduce the average waiting time in a queue. It also suggests that managing the arrival rate can help to control the average number of customers in a queue.

In the next section, we will explore some applications of Little's Law in logistical and transportation planning.




### Subsection: 5.1c Case Studies

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that illustrate the application of Little's Law and queueing theory in logistical and transportation planning. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts discussed in the previous sections and will help in applying them to real-world problems.

#### Case Study 1: Traffic Flow on a Highway

Consider a highway with a single lane in each direction. The average arrival rate of vehicles at the highway is $\lambda$ vehicles per hour, and the average service rate is $\mu$ vehicles per hour. The average waiting time for a vehicle to enter the highway is $W$.

Using Little's Law, we can express the average number of vehicles in the queue as:

$$
L = \lambda \cdot W
$$

If we know the average arrival rate and the average waiting time, we can calculate the average number of vehicles in the queue. This information can be used to plan for additional lanes or traffic control measures to reduce the waiting time and improve the overall traffic flow.

#### Case Study 2: Delivery Queue at a Warehouse

Consider a warehouse where deliveries are made through a single queue. The average arrival rate of deliveries is $\lambda$ deliveries per hour, and the average service rate is $\mu$ deliveries per hour. The average waiting time for a delivery to be served is $W$.

Using Little's Law, we can express the average number of deliveries in the queue as:

$$
L = \lambda \cdot W
$$

If we know the average arrival rate and the average waiting time, we can calculate the average number of deliveries in the queue. This information can be used to plan for additional service facilities or to optimize the service process to reduce the waiting time and improve the overall efficiency of the warehouse.

#### Case Study 3: Call Center Queue

Consider a call center where customers call for support. The average arrival rate of calls is $\lambda$ calls per hour, and the average service rate is $\mu$ calls per hour. The average waiting time for a call to be answered is $W$.

Using Little's Law, we can express the average number of calls in the queue as:

$$
L = \lambda \cdot W
$$

If we know the average arrival rate and the average waiting time, we can calculate the average number of calls in the queue. This information can be used to plan for additional service facilities or to optimize the service process to reduce the waiting time and improve the overall customer satisfaction.

These case studies illustrate the power of Little's Law and queueing theory in analyzing and optimizing queueing systems in logistical and transportation planning. By understanding the average arrival rate, service rate, and waiting time, we can calculate the average number of customers in the queue and use this information to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of queueing systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have introduced the concept of queues and their importance in logistical and transportation planning. We have explored the fundamental principles of queueing theory, including Little's Law, which provides a mathematical relationship between the average number of customers in a queue, the average arrival rate, and the average service time. We have also discussed the different types of queues, such as single-server queues, multiple-server queues, and finite-capacity queues, each with their unique characteristics and applications in logistical and transportation planning.

Queues play a crucial role in the efficient operation of logistical and transportation systems. They help manage the flow of customers, resources, and information, ensuring that services are delivered in a timely and effective manner. By understanding the principles of queueing theory, logistical and transportation planners can design and optimize their systems to meet the demands of their customers and stakeholders.

In the next chapter, we will delve deeper into the application of queueing theory in logistical and transportation planning, exploring advanced concepts such as queue discipline, queueing networks, and performance measures. We will also discuss real-world case studies and examples to illustrate the practical application of these concepts.

## Chapter: Chapter 6: Networks and Flows

### Introduction

In the realm of logistical and transportation planning, understanding networks and flows is crucial. This chapter, "Networks and Flows," delves into the fundamental concepts and methodologies that govern the movement of goods, services, and information across various networks. 

Networks, in the context of logistics and transportation, refer to the interconnected systems that facilitate the movement of goods and services. These networks can be physical, such as road, rail, and air networks, or virtual, such as information networks. The efficiency and effectiveness of these networks significantly impact the overall logistical and transportation planning process.

Flows, on the other hand, represent the movement of goods, services, and information across these networks. They are the lifeblood of logistical and transportation planning, driving the flow of commerce and services. Understanding the dynamics of these flows, including their patterns, rates, and potential disruptions, is essential for effective planning and decision-making.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models and algorithms that describe and analyze networks and flows. We will also discuss the principles of network design and optimization, and how they can be applied to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of logistical and transportation systems. 

We will also delve into the concept of network flows, including the principles of conservation of flow and the calculation of flow rates. We will explore how these concepts can be applied to various types of networks, from physical transportation networks to virtual information networks.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and methodologies that govern networks and flows in logistical and transportation planning. They should also be able to apply these concepts to real-world problems, using the mathematical models and algorithms discussed in this chapter.




### Subsection: 5.2a Definition and Properties

The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is a type of queueing model that is used to analyze systems where the arrival and service rates are not constant but vary over time. It is a special case of the more general Markov queueing model, which is named after the Russian mathematician Andrey Kolmogorov.

#### Definition

The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is a single-server queueing model where the arrival and service rates are not constant but vary over time. The model is defined by two parameters, $\lambda(t)$ and $\mu(t)$, which represent the arrival and service rates, respectively, at time $t$. The arrival rate $\lambda(t)$ is the rate at which customers enter the queue, and the service rate $\mu(t)$ is the rate at which customers are served.

The model is named "birth-and-death" because the arrival and service rates can be thought of as the "birth" and "death" rates of customers in the queue. When the arrival rate is high, more customers enter the queue, and the queue length increases (birth). When the service rate is high, more customers are served, and the queue length decreases (death).

#### Properties

The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue has several important properties that make it a useful tool for analyzing queueing systems. These properties include:

1. The queue length is a Markov chain: The queue length in the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is a Markov chain, meaning that the future state of the queue depends only on its current state and not on its past states. This property simplifies the analysis of the queue, as it allows us to use the powerful tools of Markov chain theory.

2. The queue length is ergodic: The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is ergodic, meaning that its long-term behavior is the same as its short-term behavior. This property is important for understanding the stability of the queue.

3. The queue length is stationary: The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue has a unique stationary distribution, meaning that the queue length will eventually settle down to a steady-state value. This property is useful for predicting the long-term behavior of the queue.

4. The queue length is bounded: The queue length in the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is always finite, meaning that the queue will never grow infinitely large. This property is important for understanding the capacity of the queue.

5. The queue length is sensitive to changes in the arrival and service rates: The queue length in the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is highly sensitive to changes in the arrival and service rates. Small changes in these rates can lead to large changes in the queue length, making the queue a useful tool for understanding the impact of changes in the system.

In the next section, we will explore how to use the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue to analyze queueing systems in more detail.




### Subsection: 5.2b Applications in Logistics

The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue has a wide range of applications in logistics. In this section, we will explore some of these applications and how the properties of the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue make it a valuable tool for analyzing logistical systems.

#### Supply Chain Management

One of the most common applications of the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue in logistics is in supply chain management. Supply chains are complex systems that involve the movement of goods from suppliers to manufacturers to retailers. The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue can be used to model the flow of goods through this system, taking into account the varying arrival and service rates at different stages of the supply chain.

For example, consider a supply chain with three stages: supplier, manufacturer, and retailer. The arrival rate at the supplier is high, as they receive goods from multiple suppliers. The service rate at the supplier is also high, as they need to process and ship the goods quickly. However, the arrival rate at the manufacturer is lower, as they only receive goods from the supplier. The service rate at the manufacturer is also lower, as they need to process the goods more carefully. Finally, the arrival rate at the retailer is even lower, as they only receive goods from the manufacturer. The service rate at the retailer is also lower, as they need to process and ship the goods to customers.

Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, we can model the queueing system at each stage of the supply chain and analyze its performance. This can help us identify potential bottlenecks and improve the overall efficiency of the supply chain.

#### Transportation Planning

Another important application of the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue in logistics is in transportation planning. Transportation planning involves the management of the movement of people and goods through a transportation system. The Markov Birth-and-Death Queue can be used to model the flow of traffic through this system, taking into account the varying arrival and service rates at different stages of the transportation system.

For example, consider a transportation system with three stages: roads, highways, and interstates. The arrival rate at the roads is high, as they receive traffic from multiple sources. The service rate at the roads is also high, as they need to process and move the traffic quickly. However, the arrival rate at the highways is lower, as they only receive traffic from the roads. The service rate at the highways is also lower, as they need to process the traffic more carefully. Finally, the arrival rate at the interstates is even lower, as they only receive traffic from the highways. The service rate at the interstates is also lower, as they need to process and move the traffic to their destinations.

Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, we can model the queueing system at each stage of the transportation system and analyze its performance. This can help us identify potential bottlenecks and improve the overall efficiency of the transportation system.

#### Conclusion

In conclusion, the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue is a powerful tool for analyzing queueing systems in logistics. Its properties make it a valuable tool for understanding the behavior of complex systems and improving their performance. By using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, we can gain insights into the flow of goods and traffic through logistical systems and make informed decisions to improve their efficiency.





### Subsection: 5.2c Examples and Exercises

In this section, we will provide some examples and exercises to help you better understand the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue and its applications in logistics.

#### Example 1: Supply Chain Management

Consider the supply chain system described in the previous section. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, we can model the queueing system at each stage of the supply chain and analyze its performance. Let's assume the following parameters for each stage:

- Supplier: arrival rate = 10, service rate = 15
- Manufacturer: arrival rate = 5, service rate = 10
- Retailer: arrival rate = 2, service rate = 8

Using Little's Law, we can calculate the average number of customers in the system at each stage:

- Supplier: $L = \frac{\lambda}{\mu} = \frac{10}{15} = 0.67$
- Manufacturer: $L = \frac{\lambda}{\mu} = \frac{5}{10} = 0.5$
- Retailer: $L = \frac{\lambda}{\mu} = \frac{2}{8} = 0.25$

We can also calculate the average waiting time for each stage:

- Supplier: $W = \frac{L}{\lambda} = \frac{0.67}{10} = 0.067$ minutes
- Manufacturer: $W = \frac{L}{\lambda} = \frac{0.5}{5} = 0.1$ minutes
- Retailer: $W = \frac{L}{\lambda} = \frac{0.25}{2} = 0.125$ minutes

This allows us to identify potential bottlenecks in the supply chain and make improvements to increase efficiency.

#### Example 2: Transportation Planning

In transportation planning, the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue can be used to model the flow of vehicles through a transportation system. Let's consider a highway system with two lanes in each direction. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, we can model the queueing system at each lane and analyze its performance. Let's assume the following parameters for each lane:

- Arrival rate: 1000 vehicles per hour
- Service rate: 2000 vehicles per hour

Using Little's Law, we can calculate the average number of vehicles in the system at each lane:

- Lane 1: $L = \frac{\lambda}{\mu} = \frac{1000}{2000} = 0.5$
- Lane 2: $L = \frac{\lambda}{\mu} = \frac{1000}{2000} = 0.5$

We can also calculate the average waiting time for each lane:

- Lane 1: $W = \frac{L}{\lambda} = \frac{0.5}{1000} = 0.0005$ hours
- Lane 2: $W = \frac{L}{\lambda} = \frac{0.5}{1000} = 0.0005$ hours

This allows us to identify potential congestion points in the transportation system and make improvements to increase efficiency.

#### Exercise 1

Consider a supply chain system with three stages: supplier, manufacturer, and retailer. The arrival rate at the supplier is 10, the service rate at the supplier is 15, the arrival rate at the manufacturer is 5, and the service rate at the manufacturer is 10. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, calculate the average number of customers in the system at each stage and the average waiting time for each stage.

#### Exercise 2

Consider a transportation system with two lanes in each direction. The arrival rate for each lane is 1000 vehicles per hour and the service rate for each lane is 2000 vehicles per hour. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, calculate the average number of vehicles in the system at each lane and the average waiting time for each lane.

#### Exercise 3

Consider a supply chain system with four stages: supplier, manufacturer, wholesaler, and retailer. The arrival rate at the supplier is 10, the service rate at the supplier is 15, the arrival rate at the manufacturer is 5, the service rate at the manufacturer is 10, the arrival rate at the wholesaler is 2, and the service rate at the wholesaler is 8. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, calculate the average number of customers in the system at each stage and the average waiting time for each stage.

#### Exercise 4

Consider a transportation system with three lanes in each direction. The arrival rate for each lane is 1500 vehicles per hour and the service rate for each lane is 3000 vehicles per hour. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, calculate the average number of vehicles in the system at each lane and the average waiting time for each lane.

#### Exercise 5

Consider a supply chain system with five stages: supplier, manufacturer, wholesaler, retailer, and customer. The arrival rate at the supplier is 10, the service rate at the supplier is 15, the arrival rate at the manufacturer is 5, the service rate at the manufacturer is 10, the arrival rate at the wholesaler is 2, the service rate at the wholesaler is 8, the arrival rate at the retailer is 1, and the service rate at the retailer is 4. Using the Markov Birth-and-Death Queue, calculate the average number of customers in the system at each stage and the average waiting time for each stage.

### Section: 6.1 Some Transportation Problems:

Transportation planning is a complex and multifaceted process that involves the analysis of various factors such as traffic patterns, transportation infrastructure, and economic considerations. In this section, we will explore some of the common transportation problems that arise in logistical planning and the methods used to solve them.

#### 6.1a Problem Definition

Before delving into the specific transportation problems, it is important to understand the general problem definition. The goal of transportation planning is to create efficient and sustainable transportation systems that meet the needs of individuals, businesses, and society as a whole. However, there are often conflicting interests and limited resources that make this task challenging.

One of the key aspects of problem definition is identifying the decision variables. These are the factors that can be controlled or manipulated to solve the problem. In transportation planning, decision variables may include the location of transportation infrastructure, the type of transportation services provided, and the pricing of transportation services.

Another important aspect of problem definition is understanding the objectives and constraints of the problem. The objectives of transportation planning may include minimizing travel time, reducing traffic congestion, and promoting sustainable transportation options. Constraints may include limited funding, environmental regulations, and competing interests of different stakeholders.

Once the problem has been defined, various methods can be used to solve it. These methods may involve mathematical modeling, optimization, and simulation techniques. The choice of method depends on the specific problem and the available data and resources.

In the following sections, we will explore some of the common transportation problems and the methods used to solve them. These include traffic congestion, transportation infrastructure planning, and transportation equity. By understanding the problem definition and the various methods used to solve them, readers will gain a comprehensive understanding of transportation planning and its role in logistics.





#### 6.1b Solution Approaches

Once a transportation problem has been defined, there are various methods that can be used to solve it. These methods may involve mathematical modeling, optimization, and simulation techniques. The choice of method depends on the specific problem and the available data and resources.

##### Mathematical Modeling

Mathematical modeling is a powerful tool for solving transportation problems. It involves creating a mathematical representation of the problem, which can then be solved using various techniques such as linear programming, nonlinear programming, and differential equations.

For example, consider the problem of traffic congestion. This can be modeled as a linear programming problem, where the decision variables are the number of lanes on a road and the traffic flow on each lane. The objective is to minimize the total travel time, subject to constraints such as the maximum capacity of each lane and the total number of lanes on the road.

##### Optimization

Optimization is another common approach to solving transportation problems. It involves finding the optimal solution to a problem, which is the best possible solution given a set of constraints. This can be done using various optimization techniques such as linear programming, nonlinear programming, and dynamic programming.

For example, consider the problem of transportation infrastructure planning. This can be formulated as a linear programming problem, where the decision variables are the location and capacity of transportation infrastructure such as roads, railways, and airports. The objective is to maximize the overall efficiency of the transportation system, subject to constraints such as budget limitations and environmental regulations.

##### Simulation

Simulation is a powerful tool for studying complex transportation systems. It involves creating a computer model of the system and running simulations to observe the behavior of the system under different scenarios. This can help identify potential problems and evaluate the effectiveness of different solutions.

For example, consider the problem of traffic flow management. This can be studied using a simulation model, where the behavior of individual vehicles is simulated based on rules such as lane changing, merging, and speed limits. This can help identify bottlenecks and inefficiencies in the traffic flow, and evaluate the effectiveness of different traffic management strategies.

In conclusion, there are various methods that can be used to solve transportation problems. The choice of method depends on the specific problem and the available data and resources. By understanding the problem definition and the available methods, transportation planners can make informed decisions to create efficient and sustainable transportation systems.





### Subsection: 6.1c Case Studies

In this section, we will explore some real-world case studies that demonstrate the application of logistical and transportation planning methods. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts discussed in the previous sections and highlight the challenges and solutions faced in the field.

#### 6.1c.1 Traffic Congestion in Urban Areas

Traffic congestion is a major transportation problem in urban areas. It not only leads to delays and increased travel time but also has negative impacts on the environment and public health. The city of Boston, USA, has been facing significant traffic congestion issues, particularly on its major highways.

To address this problem, the city has implemented a variety of transportation planning methods. These include the use of intelligent transportation systems (ITS) to collect and analyze traffic data, the implementation of congestion pricing to discourage unnecessary driving, and the development of public transportation systems to provide alternative modes of transportation.

The city has also implemented a cellular model, where multiple projects are in progress to address the traffic congestion issue. These projects include the development of a new highway system, the expansion of existing roads, and the implementation of new public transportation systems.

#### 6.1c.2 Transportation Infrastructure Planning in Developing Countries

Transportation infrastructure planning is a critical aspect of logistical and transportation planning. However, developing countries often face unique challenges in this area due to limited resources and infrastructure.

For example, the country of Ghana has been facing significant transportation challenges, particularly in terms of its road network. The country has a high density of urban areas, which puts pressure on its road infrastructure. Additionally, the country has a high rate of vehicle ownership, which further exacerbates the problem.

To address these challenges, the Ghanaian government has implemented a variety of transportation planning methods. These include the use of mathematical modeling to optimize the placement of transportation infrastructure, the implementation of traffic management systems to improve traffic flow, and the development of public transportation systems to provide alternative modes of transportation.

#### 6.1c.3 Logistics Management in the Retail Industry

Logistics management is a critical aspect of transportation planning, particularly in the retail industry. Retailers face significant challenges in managing their supply chains, which involve the movement of goods from suppliers to manufacturers to retailers.

For example, the retail giant Walmart has implemented a variety of logistics management methods to improve its supply chain efficiency. These include the use of advanced transportation planning software to optimize transportation routes and schedules, the implementation of just-in-time delivery systems to reduce inventory costs, and the development of a global logistics network to improve international trade.

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the diverse range of transportation problems faced by different entities and the various methods used to address them. They also underscore the importance of logistical and transportation planning in addressing these problems and improving overall efficiency.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored various transportation problems and the methods used to solve them. We have delved into the complexities of logistical and transportation planning, understanding the importance of efficient and effective transportation systems in various industries. We have also learned about the different types of transportation problems, such as the vehicle routing problem, the multi-modal transportation problem, and the transportation network design problem. 

We have also discussed the various methods used to solve these problems, including mathematical programming, simulation, and heuristic approaches. Each of these methods has its strengths and weaknesses, and the choice of method depends on the specific problem at hand. 

In conclusion, transportation planning is a critical aspect of logistics and supply chain management. It involves the design, management, and optimization of transportation systems to ensure the smooth movement of people and goods. The methods discussed in this chapter provide a solid foundation for understanding and solving transportation problems.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a transportation network with five cities connected by six roads. Each road has a capacity of 100 vehicles per hour. If the daily traffic demand for each city is given by the vector $d = (100, 150, 200, 120, 180)$, formulate a linear programming model to maximize the total number of vehicles that can be transported through the network.

#### Exercise 2
A company is planning to transport goods from three warehouses to four retail stores. The cost of transportation from each warehouse to each store is given by the matrix $C = 
\begin{bmatrix}
2 & 3 & 4 & 5 \\
3 & 4 & 5 & 6 \\
4 & 5 & 6 & 7
\end{bmatrix}$. If the company can only afford to spend $1000 on transportation, formulate a linear programming model to minimize the total cost of transportation while ensuring that the demand for each store is met.

#### Exercise 3
Consider a transportation network with three modes of transportation: road, rail, and air. The cost of transportation per unit of cargo is given by the vector $c = (2, 3, 4)$. If the network has a total of 1000 units of cargo to be transported, formulate a linear programming model to minimize the total cost of transportation while ensuring that the demand for each mode of transportation is met.

#### Exercise 4
A city is planning to build a new transportation network. The network will consist of a set of roads, each with a cost of construction given by the vector $c = (100, 200, 300, 400)$. If the city has a budget of $1000000, formulate a linear programming model to maximize the number of roads that can be built while staying within the budget.

#### Exercise 5
Consider a transportation network with five cities connected by six roads. Each road has a capacity of 100 vehicles per hour. If the daily traffic demand for each city is given by the vector $d = (100, 150, 200, 120, 180)$, formulate a simulation model to simulate the traffic flow on the network.

## Chapter: Chapter 7: Transportation Planning Models

### Introduction

Transportation planning is a critical aspect of urban development and management. It involves the design, implementation, and management of transportation systems to meet the needs of a community. The effectiveness of a transportation system can significantly impact the quality of life for residents, the efficiency of the economy, and the sustainability of the environment. 

In this chapter, we will delve into the various transportation planning models that are used to address the complex challenges of transportation planning. These models provide a systematic and quantitative approach to decision-making, helping planners to understand the impacts of different transportation strategies and to make informed decisions. 

We will explore the different types of transportation planning models, including network models, activity-based models, and land change models. Each of these models has its strengths and limitations, and they are often used in combination to provide a comprehensive understanding of transportation systems. 

We will also discuss the principles and techniques used in transportation planning modeling, such as land use and transportation interaction modeling (LUTIM), land change modeling, and activity-based modeling. These techniques are used to simulate the interactions between land use, transportation, and activity patterns, providing insights into the complex dynamics of transportation systems. 

Finally, we will examine the role of transportation planning models in the broader context of urban planning and policy-making. We will discuss how these models can be used to support strategic planning, policy analysis, and decision-making, and how they can contribute to the development of sustainable and equitable transportation systems.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of transportation planning models, equipping readers with the knowledge and skills to understand, apply, and critically evaluate these models in the context of urban planning and policy-making.




### Subsection: 6.2a Definition and Properties

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of Markov queues and their applications in transportation planning. In this section, we will delve deeper into the topic and explore more general Markov queues.

#### 6.2a Definition and Properties

A Markov queue is a mathematical model used to describe the behavior of a system where the future state of the system depends only on its current state and not on its past states. This property is known as the Markov property and is a fundamental concept in the study of Markov queues.

More general Markov queues are a type of Markov queue that allows for a more complex relationship between the current state and the future state of the system. In these queues, the future state of the system may depend not only on the current state but also on the past states of the system. This allows for a more realistic representation of real-world systems, where the future state may be influenced by multiple factors.

One of the key properties of Markov queues is the concept of stationarity. A queue is said to be stationary if the probability of being in a particular state at any given time is independent of the time at which the system is observed. In other words, the system is in a steady state and does not change over time.

Another important property of Markov queues is the concept of ergodicity. A queue is said to be ergodic if it is possible to observe the system for a long enough period of time and make predictions about its future behavior. This property is closely related to the concept of stationarity, as an ergodic queue is also stationary.

In the next section, we will explore the different types of more general Markov queues and their applications in transportation planning. We will also discuss the concept of traffic flow and its relationship with Markov queues.





### Subsection: 6.2b Applications in Transportation Planning

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of more general Markov queues and their properties. In this section, we will explore some of the applications of these queues in transportation planning.

#### 6.2b Applications in Transportation Planning

Transportation planning is a crucial aspect of urban development and management. It involves the design and management of transportation systems to meet the needs of a city's population. With the increasing population and urbanization, transportation planning has become more complex and challenging. This is where more general Markov queues can be useful.

One of the key applications of more general Markov queues in transportation planning is in traffic flow modeling. Traffic flow refers to the movement of vehicles on a road network. It is a complex system that is influenced by various factors such as road conditions, weather, and driver behavior. More general Markov queues can be used to model traffic flow by considering the current state of the system and its past states. This allows for a more realistic representation of traffic flow, taking into account the dynamic nature of the system.

Another important application of more general Markov queues in transportation planning is in the design of transportation networks. Transportation networks refer to the interconnected system of roads, highways, and other modes of transportation that connect different parts of a city. More general Markov queues can be used to model the behavior of these networks and predict their performance under different scenarios. This can help transportation planners make informed decisions about the design and management of these networks.

More general Markov queues can also be used in transportation planning to analyze the impact of different policies and interventions. For example, transportation planners can use these queues to model the effects of implementing a new public transportation system or changing traffic patterns. This can help them evaluate the effectiveness of these policies and make adjustments as needed.

In addition to these applications, more general Markov queues can also be used in transportation planning to study the behavior of different modes of transportation. This includes analyzing the flow of pedestrians, cyclists, and public transportation users. By considering the current state and past states of these systems, more general Markov queues can provide valuable insights into their behavior and help transportation planners make informed decisions.

In conclusion, more general Markov queues have a wide range of applications in transportation planning. From traffic flow modeling to network design and policy analysis, these queues can provide valuable insights into the complex and dynamic nature of transportation systems. As transportation planning continues to evolve and become more complex, the use of more general Markov queues will become even more crucial in addressing the challenges and improving the efficiency of transportation systems.





#### 6.2c Examples and Exercises

In this section, we will provide some examples and exercises to help you better understand the concepts discussed in this chapter.

##### Example 1: Traffic Flow Modeling

Consider a road network with three intersections. The current state of the system is represented by the vector <math>\mathbf{x} = [x_1, x_2, x_3]^T</math>, where <math>x_i</math> represents the number of vehicles at intersection <math>i</math>. The transition matrix <math>\mathbf{P}</math> is given by:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.8 & 0.1 & 0.1 \\
0.2 & 0.6 & 0.2 \\
0.3 & 0.3 & 0.4
\end{bmatrix}
$$

Using this information, we can model the traffic flow on this road network using a more general Markov queue.

##### Example 2: Design of Transportation Networks

Consider a transportation network with four modes of transportation: car, bus, train, and subway. The current state of the system is represented by the vector <math>\mathbf{x} = [x_1, x_2, x_3, x_4]^T</math>, where <math>x_i</math> represents the number of users of mode <math>i</math>. The transition matrix <math>\mathbf{P}</math> is given by:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.6 & 0.2 & 0.1 & 0.1 \\
0.3 & 0.4 & 0.2 & 0.1 \\
0.2 & 0.3 & 0.3 & 0.2 \\
0.1 & 0.2 & 0.3 & 0.4
\end{bmatrix}
$$

Using this information, we can model the behavior of this transportation network using a more general Markov queue.

##### Exercise 1: Traffic Flow Analysis

Consider a road network with two intersections. The current state of the system is represented by the vector <math>\mathbf{x} = [x_1, x_2]^T</math>, where <math>x_i</math> represents the number of vehicles at intersection <math>i</math>. The transition matrix <math>\mathbf{P}</math> is given by:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.7 & 0.3 \\
0.4 & 0.6
\end{bmatrix}
$$

Using this information, analyze the traffic flow on this road network using a more general Markov queue.

##### Exercise 2: Impact of Policy Interventions

Consider a transportation network with three modes of transportation: car, bus, and train. The current state of the system is represented by the vector <math>\mathbf{x} = [x_1, x_2, x_3]^T</math>, where <math>x_i</math> represents the number of users of mode <math>i</math>. The transition matrix <math>\mathbf{P}</math> is given by:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \begin{bmatrix}
0.6 & 0.2 & 0.2 \\
0.3 & 0.4 & 0.3 \\
0.2 & 0.3 & 0.5
\end{bmatrix}
$$

Using this information, analyze the impact of implementing a new public transportation policy on the behavior of this transportation network using a more general Markov queue.

As a transportation planner, how would you address these concerns and ensure that the highway is built in a sustainable and environmentally friendly manner?

It is a complex and dynamic field that requires a deep understanding of various factors such as supply chain management, inventory management, transportation modes, and transportation networks. This chapter will cover all these aspects and more, providing readers with a comprehensive understanding of logistical and transportation planning.

The chapter will begin by discussing the basics of logistical and transportation planning, including its definition, objectives, and key components. Queueing theory is a mathematical framework used to model and analyze systems where customers or jobs arrive, wait in a queue, and are eventually served. It has been widely applied in various industries, including transportation and logistics, to optimize system performance and improve customer experience. The M/G/1 queue is a simple yet powerful model that is commonly used to study queueing systems. It assumes that customers arrive at a single queue according to a Poisson process, are served by a single server, and have a general service time distribution.

In this chapter, we will explore the M/G/1 queue in detail, including its assumptions, characteristics, and performance measures. ## Chapter 7: The M/G/1 Queue and Extensions:




### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored various methods and techniques for logistical and transportation planning. We have discussed the importance of efficient and effective planning in the transportation and logistics industry, and how it can lead to improved customer satisfaction, reduced costs, and increased productivity. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the M/G/1 queue and its extensions, which are fundamental concepts in the field of queueing theory.

Queueing theory is a mathematical framework used to model and analyze systems where customers or jobs arrive, wait in a queue, and are eventually served. It has been widely applied in various industries, including transportation and logistics, to optimize system performance and improve customer experience. The M/G/1 queue is a simple yet powerful model that is commonly used to study queueing systems. It assumes that customers arrive at a single queue according to a Poisson process, are served by a single server, and have a general service time distribution.

In this chapter, we will explore the M/G/1 queue in detail, including its assumptions, characteristics, and performance measures. ### Subsection: 7.1b Applications in Logistics

The M/G/1 queue and its extensions have been widely used in logistics to model and analyze various systems. In this section, we will explore some of the applications of these queueing models in logistics.

#### Warehouse Automation

One of the key applications of the M/G/1 queue and its extensions is in warehouse automation. Warehouses are complex systems where goods are received, stored, and shipped out. The M/G/1 queue can be used to model the arrival and service processes in a warehouse, allowing for the optimization of system performance.

For example, consider a warehouse with a single server and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution can be modeled using a general distribution, such as a normal or exponential distribution, to account for variations in the time it takes to process a shipment. By analyzing the queue, we can determine the average number of customers in the system, the average waiting time, and the average queue length. This information can then be used to make decisions about the number of servers and the service time distribution to optimize system performance.

#### Transportation Networks

The M/G/1 queue and its extensions have also been used to model transportation networks, such as highways and railways. These networks are complex systems where vehicles travel from one location to another, encountering delays and bottlenecks along the way. The M/G/1 queue can be used to model the arrival and service processes in these networks, allowing for the optimization of system performance.

For example, consider a highway with a single lane and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution can be modeled using a general distribution, such as a normal or exponential distribution, to account for variations in travel time due to traffic and road conditions. By analyzing the queue, we can determine the average number of vehicles in the system, the average waiting time, and the average queue length. This information can then be used to make decisions about the number of lanes and the service time distribution to optimize system performance.

#### Supply Chain Management

Supply chain management is another area where the M/G/1 queue and its extensions have been applied. Supply chains involve the movement of goods from suppliers to manufacturers to retailers, and delays and bottlenecks can have a significant impact on system performance. The M/G/1 queue can be used to model the arrival and service processes in a supply chain, allowing for the optimization of system performance.

For example, consider a supply chain with a single supplier and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution can be modeled using a general distribution, such as a normal or exponential distribution, to account for variations in lead time due to transportation and production delays. By analyzing the queue, we can determine the average number of orders in the system, the average waiting time, and the average queue length. This information can then be used to make decisions about the number of suppliers and the service time distribution to optimize system performance.

In conclusion, the M/G/1 queue and its extensions have been widely used in logistics to model and analyze various systems. By understanding the arrival and service processes, we can optimize system performance and improve overall efficiency. 


### Conclusion

We have also discussed the extensions of the M/G/1 queue, such as the M/G/k queue and the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers, which allow for more complex and realistic models to be used in planning and decision-making.

The M/G/1 queue and its extensions are powerful tools for understanding and optimizing logistical and transportation systems. By using these models, we can gain insights into the behavior of queues and make informed decisions about resource allocation, capacity planning, and service level improvements. However, it is important to note that these models are simplifications of real-world systems and should be used with caution. Further research and analysis are often necessary to fully understand and optimize complex logistical and transportation systems.

## Chapter 8: Inventory Management:




### Subsection: 7.1c Examples and Exercises

In this section, we will provide some examples and exercises to help you apply the concepts learned in this chapter.

#### Example 1: Warehouse Automation

Consider a warehouse with a single server and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution is modeled using a normal distribution with a mean of 5 minutes and a standard deviation of 2 minutes. The arrival rate is 10 customers per hour.

Using Little's Law, we can calculate the average number of customers in the system as:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $\lambda$ is the arrival rate and $W$ is the average waiting time. Since the arrival rate is 10 customers per hour and the average waiting time is unknown, we can rearrange the equation to solve for $W$:

$$
W = \frac{L}{\lambda}
$$

Substituting the average number of customers in the system, we get:

$$
W = \frac{L}{10}
$$

To find the average waiting time, we can use the formula for the average waiting time in a single-server queue:

$$
W = \frac{\lambda}{\mu - \lambda}
$$

where $\mu$ is the service rate. Since the service rate is 1/5 minutes (or 20 customers per hour), we get:

$$
W = \frac{10}{20 - 10} = 0.5 \text{ minutes}
$$

Therefore, the average number of customers in the system is 5 customers.

#### Example 2: Transportation Networks

Consider a highway with a single lane and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution is modeled using an exponential distribution with a mean of 3 minutes. The arrival rate is 20 vehicles per hour.

Using Little's Law, we can calculate the average number of vehicles in the system as:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $\lambda$ is the arrival rate and $W$ is the average waiting time. Since the arrival rate is 20 vehicles per hour and the average waiting time is unknown, we can rearrange the equation to solve for $W$:

$$
W = \frac{L}{\lambda}
$$

Substituting the average number of vehicles in the system, we get:

$$
W = \frac{L}{20}
$$

To find the average waiting time, we can use the formula for the average waiting time in a single-server queue:

$$
W = \frac{\lambda}{\mu - \lambda}
$$

where $\mu$ is the service rate. Since the service rate is 1/3 minutes (or 20 vehicles per hour), we get:

$$
W = \frac{20}{20 - 20} = 1 \text{ minute}
$$

Therefore, the average number of vehicles in the system is 4 vehicles.

#### Exercise 1

Consider a warehouse with two servers and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution is modeled using a normal distribution with a mean of 3 minutes and a standard deviation of 1 minute. The arrival rate is 15 customers per hour.

Using Little's Law, calculate the average number of customers in the system and the average waiting time.

#### Exercise 2

Consider a transportation network with two lanes and a Poisson arrival process. The service time distribution is modeled using an exponential distribution with a mean of 2 minutes. The arrival rate is 25 vehicles per hour.

Using Little's Law, calculate the average number of vehicles in the system and the average waiting time.

The M/G/1 queue is a fundamental model in queueing theory, which is the study of waiting lines. It is a single-server queueing system, where customers arrive at a rate of $\lambda$ customers per hour and are served at a rate of $\mu$ customers per hour. This model is widely used in various industries, such as telecommunication networks, call centers, and transportation systems.

In this chapter, we will explore the different extensions of the M/G/1 queue, which allow us to model more complex systems. These extensions include the M/G/k queue, where there are $k$ servers, and the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers, where each server has a different service rate. We will also discuss the applications of these extensions in logistical and transportation planning.

Furthermore, we will cover the performance measures of the M/G/1 queue and its extensions, such as the average number of customers in the system, the average waiting time, and the average queue length. These measures are crucial for evaluating the efficiency and effectiveness of logistical and transportation systems.

## Chapter 8: The M/G/1 Queue and Extensions:




### Subsection: 7.2a Quiz Instructions

- The extensions of the M/G/1 queue model, including the M/G/k queue and the M/M/c queue

Good luck on the quiz!

### Subsection: 7.2b Quiz Review

Before we dive into the quiz, let's review some of the key concepts that will be covered in the quiz.

#### The M/G/1 Queue Model

The M/G/1 queue model is a single-server queueing model where customers arrive according to a Poisson process with rate $\lambda$ and service times are independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) according to a general distribution $G$. The model assumes that the service time is independent of the number of customers in the system and that there is no queue discipline (i.e., customers are served in the order of arrival).

#### Little's Law

Little's Law is a fundamental law in queueing theory that relates the average number of customers in the system, the average arrival rate, and the average waiting time. It states that the average number of customers in the system is equal to the average arrival rate times the average waiting time. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $\lambda$ is the average arrival rate, and $W$ is the average waiting time.

#### Markov Chains in Queueing Theory

Markov chains are a powerful tool in queueing theory for analyzing the behavior of queueing systems. They allow us to model the system as a sequence of states, where each state represents the number of customers in the system. The transitions between states represent the arrival and service events. By studying the transition probabilities, we can gain insights into the behavior of the queueing system.

#### Extensions of the M/G/1 Queue Model

The M/G/1 queue model is a fundamental model in queueing theory, but it has several extensions that allow us to model more complex systems. The M/G/k queue model allows for multiple servers, while the M/M/c queue model allows for a finite number of customers in the system. These extensions can provide a more accurate representation of real-world systems.

Now that we have reviewed these key concepts, let's put our knowledge to the test with the quiz. Good luck!

### Subsection: 7.2c Quiz Answers and Explanations

In this section, we will provide the answers and explanations for the quiz. Make sure to review these answers to solidify your understanding of the concepts covered in the quiz.

#### Quiz Answers

1. The M/G/1 queue model is a single-server queueing model where customers arrive according to a Poisson process with rate $\lambda$ and service times are independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) according to a general distribution $G$. The model assumes that the service time is independent of the number of customers in the system and that there is no queue discipline (i.e., customers are served in the order of arrival).

2. Little's Law is a fundamental law in queueing theory that relates the average number of customers in the system, the average arrival rate, and the average waiting time. It states that the average number of customers in the system is equal to the average arrival rate times the average waiting time. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $\lambda$ is the average arrival rate, and $W$ is the average waiting time.

3. Markov chains are a powerful tool in queueing theory for analyzing the behavior of queueing systems. They allow us to model the system as a sequence of states, where each state represents the number of customers in the system. The transitions between states represent the arrival and service events. By studying the transition probabilities, we can gain insights into the behavior of the queueing system.

4. The M/G/k queue model allows for multiple servers, while the M/M/c queue model allows for a finite number of customers in the system. These extensions can provide a more accurate representation of real-world systems.

#### Explanations

1. The M/G/1 queue model is a fundamental model in queueing theory. It is a single-server queueing model, meaning that there is only one server available to serve customers. Customers arrive according to a Poisson process with rate $\lambda$, and the service times are independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) according to a general distribution $G$. This model assumes that the service time is independent of the number of customers in the system and that there is no queue discipline, meaning that customers are served in the order of arrival.

2. Little's Law is a fundamental law in queueing theory that relates the average number of customers in the system, the average arrival rate, and the average waiting time. This law is essential in understanding the behavior of queueing systems. It states that the average number of customers in the system is equal to the average arrival rate times the average waiting time. This can be mathematically expressed as:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $\lambda$ is the average arrival rate, and $W$ is the average waiting time.

3. Markov chains are a powerful tool in queueing theory for analyzing the behavior of queueing systems. They allow us to model the system as a sequence of states, where each state represents the number of customers in the system. The transitions between states represent the arrival and service events. By studying the transition probabilities, we can gain insights into the behavior of the queueing system.

4. The M/G/k queue model and the M/M/c queue model are extensions of the M/G/1 queue model. The M/G/k queue model allows for multiple servers, while the M/M/c queue model allows for a finite number of customers in the system. These extensions can provide a more accurate representation of real-world systems.

#### Quiz Question 1

Consider a single-server queueing system with arrival rate $\lambda = 5$ customers per hour and service time distribution $G$ with mean 2 minutes. What is the average number of customers in the system according to Little's Law?

1. 10 customers
2. 20 customers
3. 25 customers
4. 30 customers

#### Quiz Question 2

In a single-server queueing system, customers are served in the order of arrival. If there are 10 customers in the system and the next customer has a service time of 3 minutes, what is the average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system?

1. 1 minute
2. 2 minutes
3. 3 minutes
4. 4 minutes

#### Quiz Question 3

Consider a single-server queueing system with arrival rate $\lambda = 10$ customers per hour and service time distribution $G$ with mean 1 minute. If the system is in a steady state, what is the probability that there are more than 5 customers in the system?

1. 0.1
2. 0.2
3. 0.3
4. 0.4

#### Quiz Question 4

In a single-server queueing system, customers are served in the order of arrival. If there are 10 customers in the system and the next customer has a service time of 2 minutes, what is the average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system?

1. 1 minute
2. 2 minutes
3. 3 minutes
4. 4 minutes

#### Quiz Question 5

Consider a single-server queueing system with arrival rate $\lambda = 15$ customers per hour and service time distribution $G$ with mean 1.5 minutes. If the system is in a steady state, what is the probability that there are more than 8 customers in the system?

2. 0.3
3. 0.4
4. 0.5

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Consider a single-server queueing system with arrival rate $\lambda = 5$ customers per hour and service time distribution $G$ with mean 2 minutes. Use Little's Law to calculate the average number of customers in the system.

In a single-server queueing system, customers are served in the order of arrival. If there are 10 customers in the system and the next customer has a service time of 2 minutes, what is the average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system?

#### Exercise 5
Consider a single-server queueing system with arrival rate $\lambda = 15$ customers per hour and service time distribution $G$ with mean 1.5 minutes. If the system is in a steady state, what is the probability that there are more than 8 customers in the system?

## Chapter: Chapter 8: The M/G/1 Queue with Multiple Servers

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored various aspects of queueing theory, including single-server queues and extensions of the M/G/1 queue. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the world of queueing theory by introducing the concept of the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers. 

The M/G/1 queue is a fundamental model in queueing theory, where customers arrive at a single queue and are served by a single server. However, in many real-world scenarios, there are multiple servers available to serve the customers. This leads to the need for a more complex model that can handle multiple servers. 

The M/G/1 queue with multiple servers is a natural extension of the M/G/1 queue. It allows us to model systems where there are multiple servers available to serve the customers. This model is particularly useful in scenarios where the arrival rate of customers is high, and multiple servers are necessary to handle the load. 

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical foundations of the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers. We will start by introducing the model and its assumptions. We will then discuss the key performance measures of the queue, such as the average number of customers in the system and the average waiting time. We will also explore the concept of Little's Law, which provides a relationship between the average number of customers in the system and the average waiting time. 

Furthermore, we will discuss the steady-state analysis of the queue, which allows us to calculate the long-term behavior of the queue. We will also explore the concept of queue discipline, which determines the order in which customers are served. 

Finally, we will discuss some practical applications of the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers, such as call centers and network traffic. We will also touch upon some extensions of the model, such as the M/G/k queue and the M/M/c queue. 

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the M/G/1 queue with multiple servers and its applications. You will also be equipped with the necessary tools to analyze and optimize queueing systems with multiple servers.




### Subsection: 7.2c Quiz Solutions

In this section, we will provide the solutions to the quiz questions presented in the previous section. These solutions will help us further understand the concepts and principles of the M/G/1 queue and its extensions.

#### Quiz Solution 1

The average number of customers in the system according to Little's Law can be calculated using the formula:

$$
L = \lambda W
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $\lambda$ is the arrival rate, and $W$ is the average waiting time. 

In our example, the arrival rate is $\lambda = 5$ customers per hour and the average waiting time is $W = 2$ minutes. Therefore, the average number of customers in the system is:

$$
L = 5 \times 2 = 10
$$

#### Quiz Solution 2

The average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system can be calculated using the formula:

$$
W = \frac{L - L_s}{G(s)}
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $L_s$ is the average number of customers in the system when it is full, and $G(s)$ is the service time distribution. 

In our example, the average number of customers in the system is $L = 10$ and the service time distribution is $G(s) = 2$ minutes. Therefore, the average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system is:

$$
W = \frac{10 - 10}{2} = 0
$$

This means that there is no waiting time for the remaining customers in the system.

#### Quiz Solution 3

The probability that there are more than 5 customers in the system can be calculated using the formula:

$$
P(L > 5) = 1 - P(L \leq 5)
$$

where $P(L > 5)$ is the probability that there are more than 5 customers in the system and $P(L \leq 5)$ is the probability that there are 5 or fewer customers in the system. 

In our example, the arrival rate is $\lambda = 10$ customers per hour and the service time distribution is $G(s) = 1$ minute. Therefore, the probability that there are more than 5 customers in the system is:

$$
P(L > 5) = 1 - P(L \leq 5) = 1 - \frac{10 \times (1 + 1 + 1 + 1 + 1)}{10} = 0.1
$$

#### Quiz Solution 4

The average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system can be calculated using the formula:

$$
W = \frac{L - L_s}{G(s)}
$$

where $L$ is the average number of customers in the system, $L_s$ is the average number of customers in the system when it is full, and $G(s)$ is the service time distribution. 

In our example, the average number of customers in the system is $L = 10$ and the service time distribution is $G(s) = 2$ minutes. Therefore, the average waiting time for the remaining customers in the system is:

$$
W = \frac{10 - 10}{2} = 0
$$

This means that there is no waiting time for the remaining customers in the system.

#### Quiz Solution 5

The probability that there are more than 8 customers in the system can be calculated using the formula:

$$
P(L > 8) = 1 - P(L \leq 8)
$$

where $P(L > 8)$ is the probability that there are more than 8 customers in the system and $P(L \leq 8)$ is the probability that there are 8 or fewer customers in the system. 

In our example, the arrival rate is $\lambda = 15$ customers per hour and the service time distribution is $G(s) = 1.5$ minutes. Therefore, the probability that there are more than 8 customers in the system is:

$$
P(L > 8) = 1 - P(L \leq 8) = 1 - \frac{15 \times (1 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5 + 1.5)}{15} = 0.2
$$




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the M/G/1 queue and its extensions, which are fundamental concepts in logistical and transportation planning. We have learned that the M/G/1 queue is a single-server queueing system with memoryless arrivals and general service time distribution. We have also seen how this model can be extended to include multiple servers and different service time distributions, making it a versatile tool for analyzing various real-world scenarios.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the trade-offs between queue length and waiting time. As we have seen, increasing the number of servers can reduce the queue length, but it may also lead to longer waiting times. On the other hand, using a service time distribution that is more concentrated around the mean can reduce waiting times, but it may also result in longer queue lengths.

Another important concept we have explored is the Little's Law, which relates the average queue length to the average waiting time and arrival rate. This law is a powerful tool for analyzing queueing systems and can be extended to more complex systems, such as the M/G/k queue.

Overall, the M/G/1 queue and its extensions provide a solid foundation for understanding queueing systems in logistical and transportation planning. By studying these models, we can gain insights into the behavior of real-world systems and make informed decisions to improve their performance.

#### Exercise 2
In a multi-server queueing system, each server has a service time distribution that follows a uniform distribution between 2 and 8 minutes. If there are 4 servers and the arrival rate is 20 customers per hour, what is the average queue length and waiting time for customers?

#### Exercise 3
Consider a single-server queueing system with memoryless arrivals and a service time distribution that follows a Pareto distribution with shape parameter 1.5 and scale parameter 10 minutes. If the arrival rate is 15 customers per hour, what is the average queue length and waiting time for customers?

#### Exercise 4
In a multi-server queueing system, each server has a service time distribution that follows a Weibull distribution with shape parameter 2 and scale parameter 5 minutes. If there are 3 servers and the arrival rate is 25 customers per hour, what is the average queue length and waiting time for customers?

#### Exercise 5
Consider a single-server queueing system with memoryless arrivals and a service time distribution that follows a log-normal distribution with mean 3 minutes and standard deviation 1 minute. If the arrival rate is 12 customers per hour, what is the average queue length and waiting time for customers?

## Chapter: Logistical and Transportation Planning Methods: A Comprehensive Guide

### Introduction

In today's fast-paced and interconnected world, efficient logistical and transportation planning is crucial for the smooth functioning of any organization. This chapter will delve into the concept of the M/G/k queue and its extensions, which are essential tools for understanding and analyzing queueing systems in logistical and transportation planning.

The M/G/k queue is a type of queueing system that is commonly used in logistical and transportation planning. It is a single-server queueing system with memoryless arrivals and general service time distribution. This means that the arrivals to the queue are assumed to be memoryless, meaning that the time between arrivals is independent of the previous arrivals. The service time distribution is also general, meaning that it can take on any probability distribution. This makes the M/G/k queue a versatile and powerful tool for analyzing queueing systems.

In this chapter, we will explore the various extensions of the M/G/k queue, which allow us to model more complex queueing systems. These extensions include the M/G/k/k queue, the M/G/k/kL queue, and the M/G/k/kL/kL queue. Each of these extensions adds a new level of complexity to the queueing system, allowing us to model more realistic scenarios.

We will also discuss the applications of the M/G/k queue and its extensions in logistical and transportation planning. These applications include traffic flow modeling, inventory management, and supply chain optimization. By understanding the behavior of queueing systems, we can make informed decisions and improve the efficiency of logistical and transportation processes.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to the M/G/k queue and its extensions, equipping readers with the necessary knowledge and tools to analyze and optimize queueing systems in logistical and transportation planning.
